	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Islamic calendar</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the Hijri calendar based on lunar observation. For the solar calendar whose first year is fixed to the Hijra, see <a href="/wiki/Solar_Hijri_calendar" title="Solar Hijri calendar">Solar Hijri calendar</a>. For the rule-based Hijri calendar, see <a href="/wiki/Tabular_Islamic_calendar" title="Tabular Islamic calendar">Tabular Islamic calendar</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">lunar calendar used by Muslims to determine religious observances</div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Khaled_airport_exit_stamp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/King_Khaled_airport_exit_stamp.jpg/220px-King_Khaled_airport_exit_stamp.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/King_Khaled_airport_exit_stamp.jpg/330px-King_Khaled_airport_exit_stamp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/75/King_Khaled_airport_exit_stamp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="389" data-file-height="237" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Khaled_airport_exit_stamp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Islamic Calendar stamp issued at <a href="/wiki/King_Khalid_airport" class="mw-redirect" title="King Khalid airport">King Khalid airport</a> (10 Rajab 1428 / 24 July 2007)</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Islamic</b>, <b>Muslim</b>, or <b>Hijri calendar</b> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">التقويم الهجري</span>&#8206; <i>at-taqwīm al-hijrī</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Lunar_calendar" title="Lunar calendar">lunar calendar</a> consisting of 12 <a href="/wiki/Lunar_month" title="Lunar month">lunar months</a> in a year of 354 or 355 days. It is used to determine the proper days of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_holiday" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic holiday">Islamic holidays</a> and rituals, such as the <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">annual period of fasting</a> and the proper time for the <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">pilgrimage to Mecca</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Civil_calendar" title="Civil calendar">civil calendar</a> of almost all countries where the religion is predominantly Muslim is the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a>. Notable exceptions to this rule are Iran and Afghanistan, which use the <a href="/wiki/Solar_Hijri_calendar" title="Solar Hijri calendar">Solar Hijri calendar</a>. Rents, wages and similar regular commitments are generally paid by the civil calendar.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The Islamic calendar employs the <a href="/wiki/Hijri_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Hijri era">Hijri era</a> whose <a href="/wiki/Epoch_(reference_date)" class="mw-redirect" title="Epoch (reference date)">epoch</a> was established as the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_New_Year" title="Islamic New Year">Islamic New Year</a> of 622 <a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">AD</a>/<a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> During that year, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> and his followers migrated from <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> to <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a> and established the first Muslim community (<i><a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">ummah</a></i>), an event commemorated as the <a href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hijra (Islam)">Hijra</a>. In the West, dates in this era are usually denoted <b>AH</b> (<a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Anno Hegirae</i>, "in the year of the Hijra") in parallel with the <a href="/wiki/Christian_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian era">Christian</a> (AD), <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">Common</a> (CE) and <a href="/wiki/Jewish_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish era">Jewish eras</a> (AM). In Muslim countries, it is also sometimes denoted as <b>H</b><sup id="cite_ref-WH_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WH-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> from its Arabic form (<span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">سَنة هِجْريّة</span>, abbreviated <span lang="ar" title="Arabic language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D9%87%D9%80" class="extiw" title="wikt:هـ">هـ</a></span>). In English, years prior to the Hijra are reckoned as <b>BH</b> ("Before the Hijra").<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The current Islamic year is 1441&#160;AH. In the Gregorian calendar, 1441&#160;AH runs from approximately 1&#160;September&#160;2019 to 20&#160;August&#160;2020.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Islamic_calendar">Pre-Islamic calendar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-Islamic calendar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pre-Islamic_calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Pre-Islamic calendar">Pre-Islamic calendar</a></div>
<p>For central Arabia, especially <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>, there is a lack of epigraphical evidence but details are found in the writings of Muslim authors of the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbasid">Abbasid</a> era.  Inscriptions of the ancient <a href="/wiki/South_Arabia" title="South Arabia">South Arabian</a> calendars reveal the use of a number of local calendars. At least some of these South Arabian calendars followed the <a href="/wiki/Lunisolar_calendar" title="Lunisolar calendar">lunisolar system</a>. Both <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">al-Biruni</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Mas%27udi" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Mas&#39;udi">al-Mas'udi</a> suggest that the ancient Arabs used the same month names as the Muslims, though they also record other month names used by the pre-Islamic Arabs.<sup id="cite_ref-EI2-Tarikh_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EI2-Tarikh-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Islamic tradition is unanimous in stating that Arabs of <a href="/wiki/Tihamah" title="Tihamah">Tihamah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Najd" title="Najd">Najd</a> distinguished between two types of months, permitted (<i>ḥalāl</i>) and forbidden (<i>ḥarām</i>) months.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The forbidden months were four months during which fighting is forbidden, listed as Rajab and the three months around the <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">pilgrimage</a> season, Dhu al-Qa‘dah, Dhu al-Hijjah, and Muharram. Information about the forbidden months is also found in the writings of <a href="/wiki/Procopius" title="Procopius">Procopius</a>, where he describes an armistice with the Eastern Arabs of the Lakhmid al-Mundhir which happened in the summer of 541 AD/CE.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> However, Muslim historians do not link these months to a particular season. The <a href="/wiki/Qur%27an" class="mw-redirect" title="Qur&#39;an">Qur'an</a> links the four forbidden months with <i>Nasī’</i>, a word that literally means "postponement".<sup id="cite_ref-EI2-Tarikh_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EI2-Tarikh-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> According to Muslim tradition, the decision of postponement was administered by the tribe of <a href="/wiki/Kinanah" class="mw-redirect" title="Kinanah">Kinanah</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Moberg2_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moberg2-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> by a man known as the <i>al-Qalammas</i> of Kinanah and his descendants (pl. <i>qalāmisa</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Kitab_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kitab-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Different interpretations of the concept of <i>Nasī’</i> have been proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Some scholars, both Muslim<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and Western,<sup id="cite_ref-EI2-Tarikh_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EI2-Tarikh-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moberg2_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moberg2-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> maintain that the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Islamic_Arabia" title="Pre-Islamic Arabia">pre-Islamic</a> calendar used in central Arabia was a purely lunar calendar similar to the modern Islamic calendar. According to this view, <i>Nasī’</i> is related to the pre-Islamic practices of the Meccan Arabs, where they would alter the distribution of the forbidden months within a given year without implying a calendar manipulation. This interpretation is supported by Arab historians and lexicographers, like <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Hisham" title="Ibn Hisham">Ibn Hisham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Manzur" title="Ibn Manzur">Ibn Manzur</a>, and the corpus of <a href="/wiki/Tafsir" title="Tafsir">Qur'anic exegesis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This is corroborated by an early <a href="/wiki/Sabaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Sabaic">Sabaic</a> inscription, where a religious ritual was "postponed"  (<i>ns'’w</i>) due to war. According to the context of this inscription, the verb <i>ns'’</i> has nothing to do with intercalation, but only with moving religious events within the calendar itself. The similarity between the religious concept of this ancient inscription and the Qur'an suggests that non-calendaring postponement is also the Qur'anic meaning of <i>Nasī’</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-EI2-Tarikh_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EI2-Tarikh-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclopaedia_of_Islam" title="Encyclopaedia of Islam">Encyclopaedia of Islam</a></i> concludes <i>"The Arabic system of [Nasī’] can only have been intended to move the Hajj and the fairs associated with it in the vicinity of Mecca to a suitable season of the year. It was not intended to establish a fixed calendar to be generally observed."</i><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The term "fixed calendar" is generally understood to refer to the non-intercalated calendar.
</p><p>Others concur that it was originally a lunar calendar, but suggest that about 200 years before the <a href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hijra (Islam)">Hijra</a> it was transformed into a <a href="/wiki/Lunisolar_calendar" title="Lunisolar calendar">lunisolar calendar</a> containing an <a href="/wiki/Intercalation_(timekeeping)" title="Intercalation (timekeeping)">intercalary month</a> added from time to time to keep the pilgrimage within the season of the year when merchandise was most abundant. This interpretation was first proposed by the medieval Muslim astrologer and astronomer <a href="/wiki/Abu_Ma%27shar_al-Balkhi" class="mw-redirect" title="Abu Ma&#39;shar al-Balkhi">Abu Ma'shar al-Balkhi</a>, and later by <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">al-Biruni</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Kitab_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kitab-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nasi_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nasi-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Al-Mas%27udi" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Mas&#39;udi">al-Mas'udi</a>, and some western scholars.<sup id="cite_ref-Moberg1_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moberg1-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> This interpretation considers <i>Nasī’</i> to be a synonym to the Arabic word for "intercalation" (<i>kabīsa</i>). The Arabs, according to one explanation mentioned by Abu Ma'shar, learned of this type of intercalation from the Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-Moberg2_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moberg2-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kitab_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kitab-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nasi_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nasi-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The Jewish <i>Nasi</i> was the official who decided when to intercalate the Jewish calendar.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Some sources say that the Arabs followed the Jewish practice and intercalated seven months over nineteen years, or else that they intercalated nine months over 24 years; there is, however, no consensus among scholars on this issue.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Postponement (<i>Nasī’</i>) of one ritual in a particular circumstance does not imply alteration of the sequence of months, and scholars agree that this did not happen. <a href="/wiki/Al-Biruni" title="Al-Biruni">Al-Biruni</a> also says this did not happen,<sup id="cite_ref-Nasi_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nasi-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> and the festivals were kept within their season by intercalation every second or third year of a month between Dhu al-Hijjah and Muharram. He also says that, in terms of the fixed calendar that was not introduced until 10 AH (632 AD/CE), the first intercalation was, for example, of a month between Dhu al-Hijjah and Muharram, the second of a month between Muharram and Safar, the third of a month between Safar and Rabi'I, and so on.<sup id="cite_ref-Nasi_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nasi-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The intercalations were arranged so that there were seven of them every nineteen years. The notice of intercalation was issued at the pilgrimage, the next month would be <i>Nasī’</i> and Muharram would follow. If, on the other hand, the names relate to the intercalated rather than the fixed calendar, the second intercalation might be, for example, of a month between Muharram and Safar allowing for the first intercalation, and the third intercalation of a month between Safar and Rabi'I allowing for the two preceding intercalations, and so on. The time for the intercalation to move from the beginning of the year to the end (twelve intercalations) is the time it takes the fixed calendar to revolve once through the seasons (about 32 1/2 tropical years). There are two big drawbacks of such a system, which would explain why it is not known ever to have been used anywhere in the world. First, it cannot be regulated by means of a cycle (the only cycles known in antiquity were the <a href="/wiki/Octaeteris" title="Octaeteris">octaeteris</a> (3 intercalations in 8 years) and the <a href="/wiki/Enneadecaeteris" class="mw-redirect" title="Enneadecaeteris">enneadecaeteris</a> (7 intercalations in 19 years). Secondly, without a cycle it is difficult to establish from the number of the year (a) if it is intercalary and (b) if it is intercalary, where exactly in the year the intercalation is located.
</p><p>Although some scholars (see list above) claim that the holy months were shuffled about for convenience without the use of intercalation, there is no documentary record of the festivals of any of the holy months being observed in any month other than those they are now observed in. The Qu'ran (sura 9.37) only refers to the "postponement" of a sacred month.   If they were shuffled as suggested, one would expect there to be a prohibition against "anticipation" as well. If the festivities of the sacred months were kept in season by moving them into later months, they would move through the whole twelve months in only 33 years. Had this happened, at least one writer would have mentioned it.   Sura 9.36 states "Verily, the number of months with Allah is twelve months" and sura 37 refers to "adjusting the number of months". Such adjustment can only be effected by intercalation.
</p><p>There are a number of indications that the intercalated calendar was similar to the Jewish calendar, whose year began in the spring.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> There are clues in the names of the months themselves:
</p>
<dl><dd>Rabi'I - first spring</dd>
<dd>Rabi'II - second spring</dd>
<dd>Jumada I - first month of parched land</dd>
<dd>Jumada II - second month of parched land</dd>
<dd>Sha‘bān - Arabs "dispersed" to find water</dd>
<dd>Ramadan - scorched</dd>
<dd>Shawwal - female camels "raised" their tails after calving</dd></dl>
<p>In the intercalated calendar's last year (AD/CE 632), Dhu al-Hijjah corresponded to March. The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Trench" title="Battle of the Trench">Battle of the Trench</a> in Shawwal and Dhu'l Qi'dah of AH 5 coincided with "harsh winter weather". Military campaigns clustered round Ramadan, when the summer heat had dissipated, and all fighting was forbidden during Rajab, at the height of summer. The invasion of Tabak in Rajab AH 9 was hampered by "too much hot weather" and "drought". In AH 1 Muhammad noted the Jews of Yathrib observing a festival when he arrived on Monday, 8 Rabi'I. Rabi'I is the third month and if it coincided with the third month of the Jewish calendar the festival would have been the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_Weeks" class="mw-redirect" title="Feast of Weeks">Feast of Weeks</a>, which is observed on the 6th and 7th days of that month.
</p>
<h3><span id="Prohibiting_Nas.C4.AB.E2.80.99"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Prohibiting_Nasī’">Prohibiting Nasī’</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prohibiting Nasī’">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nasi%27" title="Nasi&#39;">Nasi'</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Maome.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Maome.jpg/220px-Maome.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Maome.jpg/330px-Maome.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Maome.jpg/440px-Maome.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="360" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Maome.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Muhammad prohibiting Nasī'. Found in an illustrated copy of <a href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_Ray%E1%B8%A5%C4%81n_al-B%C4%ABr%C5%ABn%C4%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Abū Rayḥān al-Bīrūnī">Al-Bīrūnī</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Remaining_Signs_of_Past_Centuries" title="The Remaining Signs of Past Centuries">The Remaining Signs of Past Centuries</a></i> (17th-century copy of an early 14th-century <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanid</a> manuscript).<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In the tenth year of the Hijra, as documented in the <a href="/wiki/Qur%27an" class="mw-redirect" title="Qur&#39;an">Qur'an</a> (Sura <a href="/wiki/At-Tawba" title="At-Tawba">At-Tawba (9)</a>:36–37), Muslims believe <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a> revealed the "prohibition of the Nasī’".
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The number of the months, with God, is twelve in the Book of God, the day that He created the heavens and the earth; four of them are sacred. That is the right religion. So wrong not each other during them. And fight the unbelievers totally even as they fight you totally and know that God is with the godfearing.
Know that intercalation (nasi) is an addition to disbelief. Those who disbelieve are led to error thereby, making it lawful in one year and forbidden in another in order to adjust the number of (the months) made sacred by God and make the sacred ones permissible. The evil of their course appears pleasing to them. But God gives no guidance to those who disbelieve.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><i><a href="/wiki/Sura" class="mw-redirect" title="Sura">Sura</a></i>&#160;9 ("At-Tawba"), <i><a href="/wiki/Ayah" class="mw-redirect" title="Ayah">ayat</a></i>&#160;36–37<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The prohibition of Nasī’ would presumably have been announced when the intercalated month had returned to its position just before the month of Nasi' began. If Nasī' meant intercalation, then the number and the position of the intercalary months between AH 1 and AH 10 are uncertain; western calendar dates commonly cited for key events in early Islam such as the Hijra, the Battle of Badr, the Battle of Uhud and the Battle of the Trench should be viewed with caution as they might be in error by one, two, three or even four lunar months. This prohibition was mentioned by Muhammad during the <a href="/wiki/The_Farewell_Sermon" class="mw-redirect" title="The Farewell Sermon">farewell sermon</a> which was delivered on 9 Dhu al-Hijjah AH 10 (Julian date Friday 6 March, 632 AD/CE) on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Arafat" title="Mount Arafat">Mount Arafat</a> during the <a href="/wiki/The_Farewell_Pilgrimage" class="mw-redirect" title="The Farewell Pilgrimage">farewell pilgrimage</a> to Mecca.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Certainly the Nasi’ is an impious addition, which has led the infidels into error. One year they authorise the Nasi’, another year they forbid it. They observe the divine precept with respect to the number of the sacred months, but in fact they profane that which God has declared to be inviolable, and sanctify that which God has declared to be profane. Assuredly time, in its revolution, has returned to such as it was at the creation of the heavens and the earth. In the eyes of God the number of the months is twelve. Among these twelve months four are sacred, namely, Rajab, which stands alone, and three others which are consecutive.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Translated by Sherrard Beaumont Burnaby<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The three successive sacred (forbidden) months mentioned by Prophet Muhammad (months in which battles are forbidden) are <a href="/wiki/Dhu_al-Qi%27dah" class="mw-redirect" title="Dhu al-Qi&#39;dah">Dhu al-Qa‘dah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dhu_al-Hijjah" title="Dhu al-Hijjah">Dhu al-Hijjah</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Muharram" title="Muharram">Muharram</a>, months 11, 12, and 1 respectively. The single forbidden month is <a href="/wiki/Rajab" title="Rajab">Rajab</a>, month 7. These months were considered forbidden both within the new Islamic calendar and within the old pagan Meccan calendar.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Months">Months</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Months">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Four of the twelve Hijri months are considered sacred: Rajab (7), and the three consecutive months of Dhū al-Qa‘dah (11), Dhu al-Ḥijjah (12) and Muḥarram (1).<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> As the lunar calendar lags behind the solar calendar by about ten days every Gregorian year, months of the Islamic calendar fall in different parts of the Gregorian calendar each year. The cycle repeats every <a href="/wiki/List_of_Islamic_years#Modern" title="List of Islamic years">33 lunar years</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>No.</th>
<th>Name</th>
<th>Arabic</th>
<th>Meaning</th>
<th>Note
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>1</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Muharram" title="Muharram">Muharram</a></td>
<td style="direction:rtl;"><span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">مُحَرَّم</span></td>
<td>forbidden</td>
<td>A sacred month, so called because battle and all kinds of fighting are forbidden (<i>ḥarām</i>) during this month. Muḥarram includes <a href="/wiki/Day_of_Ashura" class="mw-redirect" title="Day of Ashura">‘Āshūrā’</a>, the tenth day.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>2</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Safar" title="Safar">Safar</a></td>
<td style="direction:rtl;"><span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">صَفَر</span></td>
<td>void</td>
<td>Supposedly named thus because pre-Islamic Arab houses were empty this time of year while their occupants gathered food. Another account relates that they used to loot the houses of their enemies after defeating them in battle, leaving nothing behind.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>3</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Rabi%27_al-awwal" title="Rabi&#39; al-awwal">Rabi al-Awwal</a></td>
<td style="direction:rtl;"><span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">رَبِيع ٱلْأَوَّل</span></td>
<td>the first spring</td>
<td>Also means to graze, because cattle were grazed during this month. Also a very holy month of celebration for many Muslims, as it was the month the Prophet Muhammad was born.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>4</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Rabi%27_al-thani" class="mw-redirect" title="Rabi&#39; al-thani">Rabi al-Thani</a> (Rabī’ al-Ākhir)</td>
<td style="direction:rtl;"><span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">ربيع الثاني</span> <i><b>or</b></i> <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">رَبِيع ٱلْآخِر</span></td>
<td>the second spring</td>
<td>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>5</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Jumada_al-awwal" title="Jumada al-awwal">Jumada al-Ula</a></td>
<td style="direction:rtl;"><span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">جُمَادَىٰ ٱلْأُولَىٰ</span></td>
<td>the first of parched land</td>
<td>Often considered the pre-Islamic summer. <i>Jumādá</i> may also be related to a verb meaning "to freeze" and another account relates that water would freeze during this time of year.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>6</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Jumada_al-Thani" title="Jumada al-Thani">Jumada al-Akhirah</a></td>
<td style="direction:rtl;"><span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">جُمَادَىٰ ٱلْآخِرَة</span></td>
<td>the last of parched land</td>
<td>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>7</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Rajab" title="Rajab">Rajab</a></td>
<td style="direction:rtl;"><span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">رَجَب</span></td>
<td>respect, honour</td>
<td>This is the second sacred month in which fighting is forbidden. <i>Rajab</i> may also be related to a verb meaning "to remove", so called because pre-Islamic Arabs would remove the heads of their spears and refrain from fighting.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>8</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Sha%27ban" title="Sha&#39;ban">Sha'ban</a></td>
<td style="direction:rtl;"><span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">شَعْبَان</span></td>
<td>scattered</td>
<td>Marked the time of year when Arab tribes dispersed to find water. <i>Sha‘bān</i> may also be related to a verb meaning "to be in between two things". Another account relates that it was called thus because the month lies between Rojab and Ramadan.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>9</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Ramadan_(calendar_month)" title="Ramadan (calendar month)">Ramadan</a></td>
<td style="direction:rtl;"><span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">رَمَضَان</span></td>
<td>burning heat</td>
<td>Burning is related to fasting as in empty stomach one's worldly desire will burn. Supposedly so called because of high temperatures caused by the excessive heat of the sun. Ramaḍān is the most venerated month of the Hijri calendar. During this time, Muslims must <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fast</a> from pre-dawn until sunset and should give charity to the poor and needy.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>10</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Shawwal" title="Shawwal">Shawwal</a></td>
<td style="direction:rtl;"><span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">شَوَّال</span></td>
<td>raised</td>
<td>Female <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camels</a> would normally be in calf at this time of year and raise their tails. At the first day of this month, the <a href="/wiki/Eid_al-Fitr" title="Eid al-Fitr">Eid al-Fitr</a>, "Festival of Breaking the Fast" begins, remarking the end of <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fasting</a> and the end of Ramadhan.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>11</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Dhu_al-Qi%27dah" class="mw-redirect" title="Dhu al-Qi&#39;dah">Zulqiddah</a></td>
<td style="direction:rtl;"><span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">ذُو ٱلْقَعْدَة</span></td>
<td>the one of truce/sitting</td>
<td>This is a holy month during which war is banned. People are allowed to defend themselves if attacked.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>12</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Dhu_al-Hijjah" title="Dhu al-Hijjah">Zulhijjah</a></td>
<td style="direction:rtl;"><span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">ذُو ٱلْحِجَّة</span></td>
<td>the one of pilgrimage</td>
<td>During this month Muslim pilgrims from all around the world congregate at <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> to visit the <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a>. The Hajj is performed on the eighth, ninth and the tenth of this month. <a href="/wiki/Day_of_Arafah" title="Day of Arafah">Day of Arafah</a> takes place on the ninth of the month. <a href="/wiki/Eid_al-Adha" title="Eid al-Adha">Eid al-Adha</a>, the "Festival of the Sacrifice", begins on the tenth day and ends on sunset of the twelfth, and this is a fourth holy month during which war is banned.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Length_of_months">Length of months</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Length of months">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Each month of the Islamic calendar commences on the birth of the new lunar cycle. Traditionally this is based on actual observation of the crescent (<i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/hilal#Noun" class="extiw" title="wikt:hilal">hilal</a></i>) marking the end of the previous lunar cycle and hence the previous month, thereby beginning the new month. Consequently, each month can have 29 or 30 days depending on the visibility of the moon, astronomical positioning of the earth and weather conditions. However, certain sects and groups, most notably Bohras Muslims namely <a href="/wiki/Alavi_Bohras" title="Alavi Bohras">Alavis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dawoodi_Bohra" title="Dawoodi Bohra">Dawoodis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulaymani" title="Sulaymani">Sulaymanis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shia" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia">Shia</a> <a href="/wiki/Ismaili" class="mw-redirect" title="Ismaili">Ismaili</a> Muslims, use a tabular Islamic calendar (see section below) in which odd-numbered months have thirty days (and also the twelfth month in a leap year) and even months have 29.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Days_of_the_week">Days of the week</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Days of the week">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>, the "first day" of the week corresponds with Sunday of the planetary week. The Islamic weekdays, like those in the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_calendar" title="Hebrew calendar">Hebrew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_calendar" title="Bahá&#39;í calendar">Bahá'í</a> calendars, begin at sunset. The Christian liturgical day, kept in monasteries, begins with <a href="/wiki/Vespers" title="Vespers">vespers</a> (see <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/vesper" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:vesper">vesper</a>), which is evening, in line with the other <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">Abrahamic</a> traditions. Christian and planetary weekdays begin at the following midnight. Muslims gather for worship at a mosque at noon on "gathering day" (<i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">Yawm al-Jumu‘ah</i>, <i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">yawm</i> <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">يوم</span> meaning "day") which corresponds with Friday.
</p><p>Thus "gathering day" is often regarded as the weekly day of rest. This is frequently made official, with many Muslim countries adopting Friday and Saturday (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt, Saudi Arabia</a>) or Thursday and Friday as official weekends, during which offices are closed; other countries (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>) choose to make Friday alone a day of rest. A few others (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>) have adopted the Saturday-Sunday weekend while making Friday a working day with a long midday break to allow time off for worship.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div style="overflow:auto;">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Year_numbering">Year numbering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Year numbering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hijri_year" title="Hijri year">Hijri year</a></div>
<p>In pre-Islamic Arabia, it was customary to identify a year after a major event which took place in it. Thus, according to Islamic tradition, <a href="/wiki/Abraha" title="Abraha">Abraha</a>, governor of Yemen, then a province of the Christian <a href="/wiki/Aksumite_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Aksumite Empire">Kingdom of Aksum</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>), attempted to destroy the <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a> with an army which included several elephants. The raid was unsuccessful, but that year became known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Year_of_the_Elephant" title="Year of the Elephant">Year of the Elephant</a></i>, during which Muhammad was born (sura <a href="/wiki/Al-Fil" title="Al-Fil">al-Fil</a>). Most equate this to the year 570 AD/CE, but a minority use 571 CE.
</p><p>The first ten years of the Hijra were not numbered, but were named after events in the life of Muhammad according to <a href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_Ray%E1%B8%A5%C4%81n_al-B%C4%ABr%C5%ABn%C4%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Abū Rayḥān al-Bīrūnī">Abū Rayḥān al-Bīrūnī</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>The year of permission.</li>
<li>The year of the order of fighting.</li>
<li>The year of the trial.</li>
<li>The year of congratulation on marriage.</li>
<li>The year of the earthquake.</li>
<li>The year of enquiring.</li>
<li>The year of gaining victory.</li>
<li>The year of equality.</li>
<li>The year of exemption.</li>
<li>The year of farewell.</li></ol>
<p>In AH 17 (638 AD/CE), <a href="/wiki/Abu_Musa_Ashaari" class="mw-redirect" title="Abu Musa Ashaari">Abu Musa Ashaari</a>, one of the officials of the <a href="/wiki/Caliph" class="mw-redirect" title="Caliph">Caliph</a> <a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">Umar</a> in <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basrah</a>, complained about the absence of any years on the correspondence he received from Umar, making it difficult for him to determine which instructions were most recent. This report convinced Umar of the need to introduce an era for Muslims. After debating the issue with his counsellors, he decided that the first year should be the year of Muhammad's arrival at Medina (known as Yathrib, before Muhammad's arrival). <a href="/wiki/Uthman_ibn_Affan" class="mw-redirect" title="Uthman ibn Affan">Uthman ibn Affan</a> then suggested that the months begin with Muharram, in line with the established custom of the Arabs at that time. The years of the Islamic calendar thus began with the month of Muharram in the year of Muhammad's arrival at the city of Medina, even though the actual emigration took place in Safar and Rabi' I of the intercalated calendar, two months before the commencement of Muharram in the new fixed calendar.<sup id="cite_ref-WH_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WH-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Because of the Hijra, the calendar was named the Hijri calendar.
</p><p>F A Shamsi (1984) postulated that the Arabic calendar was never intercalated. According to him, the first day of the first month of the new fixed Islamic calendar (1 Muharram AH 1) was no different from what was observed at the time. The day the Prophet moved from Quba' to Medina was originally 26 Rabi' I on the pre-Islamic calendar.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> 1 Muharram of the new fixed calendar corresponded to Friday, 16 July 622 AD/CE, the equivalent civil tabular date (same daylight period) in the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The Islamic day began at the preceding sunset on the evening of 15 July. This Julian date (16 July) was determined by <a href="/wiki/Astronomy_in_medieval_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Astronomy in medieval Islam">medieval Muslim astronomers</a> by projecting back in time their own <a href="/wiki/Tabular_Islamic_calendar" title="Tabular Islamic calendar">tabular Islamic calendar</a>, which had alternating 30- and 29-day months in each lunar year plus eleven leap days every 30 years. For example, al-Biruni mentioned this Julian date in the year 1000 AD/CE.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Although not used by either medieval Muslim astronomers or modern scholars to determine the Islamic epoch, the thin <a href="/wiki/Lunar_phase" title="Lunar phase">crescent moon</a> would have also first become visible (assuming clouds did not obscure it) shortly after the preceding sunset on the evening of 15 July, 1.5 days after the associated <a href="/wiki/Dark_moon" title="Dark moon">dark moon</a> (astronomical <a href="/wiki/New_moon" title="New moon">new moon</a>) on the morning of 14 July.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Though <a href="/wiki/Michael_Cook_(historian)" title="Michael Cook (historian)">Cook</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Crone" title="Patricia Crone">Crone</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Hagarism:_The_Making_of_the_Islamic_World" class="mw-redirect" title="Hagarism: The Making of the Islamic World">Hagarism: The Making of the Islamic World</a></i> cite a coin from AH 17, the first surviving attested use of a Hijri calendar date alongside a date in another calendar (<a href="/wiki/Coptic_calendar" title="Coptic calendar">Coptic</a>) is on a <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a> from <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> in AH 22, <a href="/wiki/PERF_558" title="PERF 558">PERF 558</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomical_considerations">Astronomical considerations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Astronomical considerations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br /><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Islamic+calendar%22">"Islamic calendar"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Islamic+calendar%22+-wikipedia">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Islamic+calendar%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Islamic+calendar%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Islamic+calendar%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Islamic+calendar%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2016</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Due to the fact that the Islamic calendar relies on certain variable methods of observation which are used to determine its month-start-dates, the start-dates of its months sometimes vary slightly from the month-start-dates of the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_calendar" title="Lunar calendar">astronomical lunar calendar</a>, which are based directly on astronomical calculations. Still, the Islamic calendar seldom varies by more than three days from the astronomical-lunar-calendar system, and roughly approximates it.  Both the Islamic calendar and the astronomical-lunar-calendar take no account of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_year" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar year">solar year</a> in their calculations, and thus both of these strictly lunar based calendar systems have no ability to reckon the timing of the four seasons of the year.
</p><p>In the astronomical-lunar-calendar system, a year of 12 lunar months is 354.37 days long.  In this calendar system, lunar months begin precisely at the time of the monthly "conjunction", when the Moon is located most directly between the Earth and the Sun. The month is defined as the average duration of a revolution of the Moon around the Earth (29.53 days). By convention, months of 30 days and 29 days succeed each other, adding up over two successive months to 59 full days. This leaves only a small monthly variation of 44 minutes to account for, which adds up to a total of 24 hours (i.e., the equivalent of one full day) in 2.73 years. To settle accounts, it is sufficient to add one day every three years to the lunar calendar, in the same way that one adds one day to the Gregorian calendar every four years.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The technical details of the adjustment are described in <a href="/wiki/Tabular_Islamic_calendar" title="Tabular Islamic calendar">Tabular Islamic calendar</a>.
</p><p>The Islamic calendar, however, is based on a different set of conventions being used for the determination of the month-start-dates.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Each month still has either 29 or 30 days, but due to the variable method of observations employed, there is usually no discernible order in the sequencing of either 29 or 30 day month lengths. Traditionally, the first day of each month is the day (beginning at sunset) of the first sighting of the hilal (crescent moon) shortly after sunset. If the hilal is not observed immediately after the 29th day of a month (either because clouds block its view or because the western sky is still too bright when the moon sets), then the day that begins at that sunset is the 30th. Such a sighting has to be made by one or more trustworthy men testifying before a committee of Muslim leaders. Determining the most likely day that the hilal could be observed was a motivation for Muslim interest in astronomy, which put Islam in the forefront of that science for many centuries.  Still, due to the fact that both lunar reckoning systems are ultimately based on the lunar cycle itself, both systems still do roughly correspond to one another, never being more than three days out of synchronisation with one another.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Clerics observe the moon.</div></div></div>
<p>This traditional practice for the determination of the start-date of the month is still followed in the overwhelming majority of Muslim countries. Each Islamic state proceeds with its own monthly observation of the new moon (or, failing that, awaits the completion of 30 days) before declaring the beginning of a new month on its territory. But, the lunar crescent becomes visible only some 17 hours after the conjunction, and only subject to the existence of a number of favourable conditions relative to weather, time, geographic location, as well as various astronomical parameters.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Given the fact that the moon sets progressively later than the sun as one goes west, with a corresponding increase in its "age" since conjunction, Western Muslim countries may, under favorable conditions, observe the new moon one day earlier than eastern Muslim countries. Due to the interplay of all these factors, the beginning of each month differs from one Muslim country to another, during the 48 hour period following the conjunction. The information provided by the calendar in any country does not extend beyond the current month.
</p><p>A number of Muslim countries try to overcome some of these difficulties by applying different astronomy-related rules to determine the beginning of months. Thus, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, and a few others begin each month at sunset on the first day that the moon sets after the sun (moonset after sunset). In Egypt, the month begins at sunset on the first day that the moon sets at least five minutes after the sun. A detailed analysis of the available data shows, however, that there are major discrepancies between what countries say they do on this subject, and what they actually do.   In some instances, what a country says it does is impossible.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to the somewhat variable nature of the Islamic calendar, in most Muslim countries, the Islamic calendar is used primarily for religious purposes, while the Solar-based Gregorian calendar is still used primarily for matters of <a href="/wiki/Commerce" title="Commerce">commerce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theological_considerations">Theological considerations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Theological considerations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>If the Islamic calendar were prepared using astronomical calculations, Muslims throughout the Muslim world could use it to meet all their needs, the way they use the Gregorian calendar today. But, there are divergent views on whether it is licit to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A majority of theologians oppose the use of calculations (beyond the constraint that each month must be not less than 29 nor more than 30 days) on the grounds that the latter would not conform with Muhammad's recommendation to observe the new moon of Ramadan and Shawal in order to determine the beginning of these months.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, some <a href="/wiki/Ulama" title="Ulama">jurists</a> see no contradiction between Muhammad's teachings and the use of calculations to determine the beginnings of lunar months.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> They consider that Muhammad's recommendation was adapted to the culture of the times, and should not be confused with the acts of worship.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thus the jurists <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Muhammad_Shakir" title="Ahmad Muhammad Shakir">Ahmad Muhammad Shakir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yusuf_al-Qaradawi" title="Yusuf al-Qaradawi">Yusuf al-Qaradawi</a> both endorsed the use of calculations to determine the beginning of all months of the Islamic calendar, in 1939 and 2004 respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> So did the <a href="/wiki/Fiqh_Council_of_North_America" title="Fiqh Council of North America">Fiqh Council of North America</a> (FCNA) in 2006<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Council_for_Fatwa_and_Research" title="European Council for Fatwa and Research">European Council for Fatwa and Research</a> (ECFR) in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The major Muslim associations of France also announced in 2012 that they would henceforth use a calendar based on astronomical calculations, taking into account the criteria of the possibility of crescent sighting in any place on Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> But, shortly after the official adoption of this rule by the <a href="/wiki/French_Council_of_the_Muslim_Faith" title="French Council of the Muslim Faith">French Council of the Muslim Faith</a> (CFCM) in 2013, the new leadership of the association decided, on the eve of Ramadan 2013, to follow the Saudi announcement rather than to apply the rule just adopted. This resulted in a division of the Muslim community of France, with some members following the new rule, and others following the Saudi announcement.
</p><p>Isma'ili-Taiyebi Bohras having the institution of <i><a href="/wiki/Da%27i_al-Mutlaq" title="Da&#39;i al-Mutlaq">da'i al-mutlaq</a></i> follow the tabular Islamic calendar (see section below) prepared on the basis of astronomical calculations from the days of <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid</a> <a href="/wiki/Imam" title="Imam">imams</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomical_12-moon_calendars">Astronomical 12-moon calendars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Astronomical 12-moon calendars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_calendar_of_Turkey">Islamic calendar of Turkey</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Islamic calendar of Turkey">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Turkish Muslims use an Islamic calendar which is calculated several years in advance (currently up to 1444 AH/2022 CE) by the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Religious_Affairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Presidency of Religious Affairs">Turkish Presidency of Religious Affairs</a> (Diyanet İşleri Başkanlığı). From 1 Muharrem 1400 AH (21 November 1979) until 29 Zilhicce 1435 (24 October 2014) the computed Turkish lunar calendar was based on the following rule: "The lunar month is assumed to begin on the evening when, within some region of the terrestrial globe, the computed centre of the lunar crescent at local sunset is more than 5° above the local horizon and (geocentrically) more than 8° from the Sun." In the current rule the (computed) lunar crescent has to be above the local horizon of <a href="/wiki/Ankara" title="Ankara">Ankara</a> at sunset.<sup id="cite_ref-turkey_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-turkey-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Saudi_Arabia.27s_Umm_al-Qura_calendar"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Saudi_Arabia's_Umm_al-Qura_calendar">Saudi Arabia's <i>Umm al-Qura</i> calendar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Saudi Arabia&#039;s Umm al-Qura calendar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> uses the sighting method to determine the beginning of each month of the Hijri calendar. Since AH 1419 (1998/99), several official hilal sighting committees have been set up by the government to determine the first visual sighting of the lunar crescent at the beginning of each lunar month. Nevertheless, the religious authorities also allow the testimony of less experienced observers and thus often announce the sighting of the lunar crescent on a date when none of the official committees could see it.
</p><p>The country also uses the Umm al-Qura calendar, based on astronomical calculations, but this is restricted to administrative purposes. The parameters used in the establishment of this calendar underwent significant changes over the past decade.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Before AH 1420 (before 18 April 1999), if the moon's age at sunset in <a href="/wiki/Riyadh" title="Riyadh">Riyadh</a> was at least 12 hours, then the day <i>ending</i> at that sunset was the first day of the month. This often caused the Saudis to celebrate holy days one or even two days before other predominantly Muslim countries, including the dates for the Hajj, which can only be dated using Saudi dates because it is performed in Mecca.
</p><p>For AH 1420–22, if moonset occurred after sunset at Mecca, then the day beginning at that sunset was the first day of a Saudi month, essentially the same rule used by Malaysia, Indonesia, and others (except for the location from which the hilal was observed).
</p><p>Since the beginning of AH 1423 (16 March 2002), the rule has been clarified a little by requiring the geocentric conjunction of the sun and moon to occur before sunset, in addition to requiring moonset to occur after sunset at Mecca. This ensures that the moon has moved past the sun by sunset, even though the sky may still be too bright immediately before moonset to actually see the crescent.
</p><p>In 2007, the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Society_of_North_America" title="Islamic Society of North America">Islamic Society of North America</a>, the <i>Fiqh</i> Council of North America and the European Council for <i><a href="/wiki/Fatwa" title="Fatwa">Fatwa</a></i> and Research announced that they will henceforth use a calendar based on calculations using the same parameters as the <i>Umm al-Qura</i> calendar to determine (well in advance) the beginning of all lunar months (and therefore the days associated with all religious observances). This was intended as a first step on the way to unify, at some future time, Muslims' calendars throughout the world.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since 1 October 2016, as a cost-cutting measure, Saudi Arabia no longer uses the Islamic calendar for paying the monthly salaries of government employees but the Gregorian calendar.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Economist171216_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist171216-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_calendars_using_the_Islamic_era">Other calendars using the Islamic era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Other calendars using the Islamic era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Solar_Hijri_calendar" title="Solar Hijri calendar">Solar Hijri calendar</a> is a solar calendar used in Iran and Afghanistan which counts its years from the <a href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hijra (Islam)">Hijra</a> or migration of Muhammad from Mecca to Medina in 622 AD/CE.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tabular_Islamic_calendar">Tabular Islamic calendar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Tabular Islamic calendar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tabular_Islamic_calendar" title="Tabular Islamic calendar">Tabular Islamic calendar</a></div>
<p>The Tabular Islamic calendar is a rule-based variation of the Islamic calendar, in which months are worked out by arithmetic rules rather than by observation or astronomical calculation. It has a 30-year cycle with 11 <a href="/wiki/Leap_year" title="Leap year">leap years</a> of 355 days and 19 years of 354 days. In the long term, it is accurate to one day in about 2,500 solar years or 2,570 lunar years. It also deviates up to about one or two days in the short term.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kuwaiti_algorithm">Kuwaiti algorithm</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Kuwaiti algorithm">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tabular_Islamic_calendar#Kuwaiti_algorithm" title="Tabular Islamic calendar">Tabular Islamic calendar §&#160;Kuwaiti algorithm</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> uses the "Kuwaiti algorithm", a variant of the tabular Islamic calendar,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> to convert Gregorian dates to the Islamic ones. Microsoft claimed that the variant is based on a statistical analysis of historical data from <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>, however it matches a known tabular calendar.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_dates">Notable dates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Notable dates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Muslim_holidays" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim holidays">Muslim holidays</a></div>
<p>Important dates in the Islamic (Hijri) year are:
</p>
<ul><li>1 Muharram: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_New_Year" title="Islamic New Year">Islamic New Year</a>.</li>
<li>10 Muharram: <a href="/wiki/Day_of_Ashura" class="mw-redirect" title="Day of Ashura">Day of Ashura</a>. For Sunnis, the crossing of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> by <a href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_Moses" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic view of Moses">Moses</a> occurred on this day. For both Shias and Sunnis, the martyrdom of <a href="/wiki/Husayn_ibn_Ali" title="Husayn ibn Ali">Husayn ibn Ali</a>, the grandson of Muhammad, and his followers.</li>
<li>12 Rabi al-Awwal: <a href="/wiki/Mawlid" title="Mawlid">Mawlid</a> or Birth of the Prophet for Sunnis.</li>
<li>17 Rabi al-Awwal: <a href="/wiki/Mawlid" title="Mawlid">Mawlid</a> for Shias.</li>
<li>27 Rajab: <a href="/wiki/Isra_and_Mi%27raj" title="Isra and Mi&#39;raj">Isra and Mi'raj</a> for the majority of Muslims.</li>
<li>15 Sha'ban: <a href="/wiki/Mid-Sha%27ban" title="Mid-Sha&#39;ban">Mid-Sha'ban</a>, or Night of Forgiveness. For Twelvers, also the birthday of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Mahdi" title="Muhammad al-Mahdi">Muhammad al-Mahdi</a>, the Twelfth Imam.</li>
<li>1 Ramadan: First day of fasting.</li>
<li>27 Ramadan: Nuzul al-Qur'an. The most probable day Muhammad received the first verses of the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>. (17 Ramadan in Indonesia and Malaysia)</li>
<li>Last third of Ramadan which includes <a href="/wiki/Laylat_al-Qadr" title="Laylat al-Qadr">Laylat al-Qadr</a>.</li>
<li>1 Shawwal: <a href="/wiki/Eid_ul-Fitr" class="mw-redirect" title="Eid ul-Fitr">Eid ul-Fitr</a>.</li>
<li>8–13 Dhu al-Hijjah: The <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a> to Mecca.</li>
<li>9 Dhu al-Hijjah: <a href="/wiki/Day_of_Arafa" class="mw-redirect" title="Day of Arafa">Day of Arafa</a>.</li>
<li>10 Dhu al-Hijjah: <a href="/wiki/Eid_al-Adha" title="Eid al-Adha">Eid al-Adha</a>.</li></ul>
<p>Days considered important predominantly for Shia Muslims:
</p>
<ul><li>9 Rabi' al-Awwal: <a href="/wiki/Eid-e-Shuja%27" title="Eid-e-Shuja&#39;">Eid-e-Shuja'</a> (Revenge taken by <a href="/wiki/Mukhtar_al-Thaqafi" title="Mukhtar al-Thaqafi">Mukhtar al-Thaqafi</a>).</li>
<li>13 Rajab: Birthday of <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali ibn Abi Talib</a></li>
<li>3 Sha'ban: Birthday of <a href="/wiki/Husayn_ibn_Ali" title="Husayn ibn Ali">Husayn ibn Ali</a>.</li>
<li>21 Ramadan: Martyrdom of Ali ibn Abi Talib.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Converting_Hijri_to_Gregorian_date_or_vice_versa">Converting Hijri to Gregorian date or vice versa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Converting Hijri to Gregorian date or vice versa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Inscription_of_years_by_A.H.%26A.D._eras.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Inscription_of_years_by_A.H.%26A.D._eras.jpg/250px-Inscription_of_years_by_A.H.%26A.D._eras.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="298" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Inscription_of_years_by_A.H.%26A.D._eras.jpg/375px-Inscription_of_years_by_A.H.%26A.D._eras.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Inscription_of_years_by_A.H.%26A.D._eras.jpg/500px-Inscription_of_years_by_A.H.%26A.D._eras.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1786" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Inscription_of_years_by_A.H.%26A.D._eras.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Civil and Hijri establishment dates of a library in Old City, Jerusalem</div></div></div>
<p>Conversions may be made by using the <a href="/wiki/Tabular_Islamic_calendar" title="Tabular Islamic calendar">Tabular Islamic calendar</a>, or, for greatest accuracy (one day in 15,186 years), via the Jewish calendar. Theoretically, the days of the months correspond in both calendars if the displacements which are a feature of the Jewish system are ignored. The table below gives, for nineteen years, the Muslim month which corresponds to the first Jewish month.
</p>
<table style="border:none;" cellspacing="0" cellpadding="0">

<tbody><tr style="vertical-align:top;">

<p>This table may be extended since every nineteen years the Muslim month number increases by seven. When it goes above twelve, subtract twelve and add one to the year AH. From 412 AD/CE to 632 AD/CE inclusive the month number is 1 and the calculation gives the month correct to a month or so. 622 AD/CE corresponds to BH 1 and AH 1. For earlier years, year BH = (623 or 622) – year AD/CE).
</p><p>An example calculation: What is the civil date and year AH of the first day of the first month in the year 20875 AD/CE?
</p><p>We first find the Muslim month number corresponding to the first month of the Jewish year which begins in 20874 AD/CE. Dividing 20874 by 19 gives quotient 1098 and remainder 12. Dividing 2026 by 19 gives quotient 106 and remainder 12. 2026 is chosen because it gives the same remainder on division by 19 as 20874. The two years are therefore (1098–106)=992×19 years apart. The Muslim month number corresponding to the first Jewish month is therefore 992×7=6944 higher than in 2026. To convert into years and months divide by twelve – 6944/12=578 years and 8 months. Adding, we get 1447y 10m + 20874y – 2026y + 578y 8m = 20874y 6m. Therefore, the first month of the Jewish year beginning in 20874 AD/CE corresponds to the sixth month of the Muslim year AH 20874. The worked example in <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_calendar#Conversion_between_Jewish_and_civil_calendars" title="Hebrew calendar">Conversion between Jewish and civil dates</a>, shows that the civil date of the first day of this month (ignoring the displacements) is Friday, 14 June. The year AH 20875 will therefore begin seven months later, on the first day of the eighth Jewish month, which the worked example shows to be 7 January, 20875 AD/CE (again ignoring the displacements). The date given by this method, being calculated, may differ by a day from the actual date, which is determined by observation.
</p><p>A reading of the section which follows will show that the year AH 20875 is wholly contained within the year 20875 AD/CE, also that in the Gregorian calendar this correspondence will occur one year earlier. The reason for the discrepancy is that the Gregorian year (like the Julian, though less so) is slightly too long, so the Gregorian date for a given AH date will be earlier and the Muslim calendar catches up sooner.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_correlations">Current correlations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Current correlations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An Islamic year will be entirely within a Gregorian year of the same number in the year 20874, after which year the number of the Islamic year will always be greater than the number of the concurrent civil year. The Islamic calendar year of 1429 occurred entirely within the civil calendar year of 2008. Such years occur once every 33 or 34 Islamic years (32 or 33 civil years). More are listed here:
</p>
<table class="wikitable" border="1" style="text-align:center;" cellpadding="2">
<tbody><tr>

<p>Because a Hijri or Islamic lunar year is between 10 and 12 days shorter than a civil year, it begins 10–12 days earlier in the civil year following the civil year in which the previous Hijri year began. Once every 33 or 34 Hijri years, or once every 32 or 33 civil years, the beginning of a Hijri year (1 Muharram) coincides with one of the first ten days of January. Subsequent Hijri New Years move backward through the civil year back to the beginning of January again, passing through each civil month from December to January.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Islamic calendar is now used primarily for religious purposes, and for official dating of public events and documents in Muslim countries. Because of its nature as a purely lunar calendar, it cannot be used for agricultural purposes and historically Islamic communities have used other calendars for this purpose: the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_calendar" title="Egyptian calendar">Egyptian calendar</a> was formerly widespread in Islamic countries, and the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_calendars" title="Iranian calendars">Iranian calendar</a> and the 1789 <a href="/wiki/Rumi_calendar" title="Rumi calendar">Ottoman calendar</a> (a modified <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>) were also used for agriculture in their countries.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> the <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a> names of the <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_calendar" title="Babylonian calendar">Babylonian calendar</a> are still used for all secular matters.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Berber_calendar" title="Berber calendar">Berber calendar</a> (another Julian calendar) is still used by farmers in the countryside.<sup id="cite_ref-EB_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> These local solar calendars have receded in importance with the near-universal adoption of the Gregorian calendar for civil purposes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Saudi Arabia uses the lunar Islamic calendar.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In Indonesia, the <a href="/wiki/Javanese_calendar" title="Javanese calendar">Javanese calendar</a>, created by <a href="/wiki/Sultan_Agung_of_Mataram" title="Sultan Agung of Mataram">Sultan Agung</a> in 1633, combines elements of the Islamic and pre-Islamic <a href="/wiki/Indian_national_calendar" title="Indian national calendar">Saka</a> calendars.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>British author <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Hagger" title="Nicholas Hagger">Nicholas Hagger</a> writes that after seizing control of <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Muammar Gaddafi">Muammar Gaddafi</a> "declared" on 1 December 1978 "that the Muslim calendar should start with the death of the prophet Mohammed in 632 rather than the hijra (Mohammed's 'emigration' from Mecca to Medina) in 622". This put the country ten solar years behind the standard Muslim calendar.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> However, according to the 2006 <i>Encyclopedia of the Developing World</i>, "More confusing still is Qaddafi's unique Libyan calendar, which counts the years from the Prophet's birth, or sometimes from his death. The months July and August, named after <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> Caesar, are now <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Nasser</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a> respectively."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Reflecting on a 2001 visit to the country, American reporter <a href="/wiki/Neil_MacFarquhar" title="Neil MacFarquhar">Neil MacFarquhar</a> observed, "Life in Libya was so unpredictable that people weren't even sure what year it was. The year of my visit was officially 1369. But just two years earlier Libyans had been living through 1429. No one could quite name for me the day the count changed, especially since both remained in play.&#160;... Event organizers threw up their hands and put the Western year in parentheses somewhere in their announcements."<sup id="cite_ref-MacFarquhar2010_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacFarquhar2010-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Computer_support">Computer support</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Islamic_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Computer support">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li>Hijri support was available in later versions of traditional <a href="/wiki/Visual_Basic" title="Visual Basic">Visual Basic</a>, and is also available in the <a href="/wiki/.NET_Framework" title=".NET Framework">.NET Framework</a>.</li>
<li>Since the release of <a href="/wiki/Java_8" class="mw-redirect" title="Java 8">Java 8</a>, the Islamic calendar is supported in the new Date and Time API.</li></ul>
